The firmware being from 2019 with published CVEs means exploit code is likely publicly available.

Domain: 2.0 Threats, Vulnerabilities, and Mitigations
Sub-objective: 2.3 — Explain various types of vulnerabilities
Question: A cloud provider's shared physical infrastructure allows a tenant's workload to potentially observe memory access patterns of another tenant's workload through CPU cache behavior. Which vulnerability type is demonstrated?
A) VM escape
B) Resource reuse vulnerability
C) Side-channel vulnerability
D) Cloud misconfiguration
Correct: C
Explanation: Observing another tenant's activity through CPU cache behavior (such as the Flush+Reload or Prime+Probe cache timing techniques) is a side-channel attack. It exploits shared physical hardware rather than a software vulnerability. VM escape requires breaking out of virtualization. Resource reuse involves uncleared data from previous use. Misconfiguration is an administrative error.

Domain: 2.0 Threats, Vulnerabilities, and Mitigations
Sub-objective: 2.4 — Given a scenario, analyze indicators of malicious activity
Question: A security analyst reviews IDS alerts showing hundreds of authentication failures from a single external IP address against an SSH service, followed by a single successful login. Which attack pattern is indicated?
A) Credential stuffing
B) Password spraying
C) Brute force attack leading to unauthorized access
D) Replay attack
Correct: C
Explanation: Hundreds of authentication failures from a single source against a single service followed by a successful login is the classic pattern of a brute force attack — systematically trying passwords until one succeeds. Credential stuffing uses known username/password pairs from data breaches (typically across multiple services, not a single SSH service). Password spraying uses one common password against many accounts. Replay attacks reuse captured valid credentials.

Domain: 2.0 Threats, Vulnerabilities, and Mitigations
Sub-objective: 2.5 — Explain the purpose of mitigation techniques used to secure the enterprise
Question: A security team implements TLS 1.3 for all web traffic, AES-256 for data at rest, and certificate-based authentication for all administrative access. Which mitigation category encompasses all of these controls?
A) Network segmentation
B) Access control
C) Encryption
D) Monitoring
Correct: C
Explanation: TLS for data in transit, AES for data at rest, and certificate-based authentication all fall under the encryption mitigation category. Encryption protects confidentiality (and in the case of certificates, authenticity). Network segmentation addresses network architecture. Access control manages who can access resources (though certificates contribute to this, the specific controls listed are encryption implementations). Monitoring is a detection control.

Domain: 2.0 Threats, Vulnerabilities, and Mitigations
Sub-objective: 2.1 — Compare and contrast common threat actors and motivations
Question: An organization's security team assesses that a persistent attacker has been silently present in their network for eight months, slowly exfiltrating intellectual property to an unknown foreign entity. Which threat actor attribute is MOST relevant to the organization's response planning?
A) The attacker's financial motivation
B) The attacker's internal vs. external classification
C) The attacker's high sophistication and persistence
D) The attacker's use of publicly available tools
Correct: C
Explanation: Understanding that the attacker is sophisticated and persistent shapes the response — standard incident response playbooks for opportunistic attacks are insufficient. The team must assume deep compromise, thorough knowledge of the environment, and that the attacker may have backdoors not yet discovered. The fact that it's been eight months of undetected access means the threat actor has significant operational security capabilities.

Domain: 2.0 Threats, Vulnerabilities, and Mitigations
Sub-objective: 2.2 — Explain common threat vectors and attack surfaces
Question: A red team exercise discovers that a significant number of employees will plug in a USB drive found in the parking lot. What is the security term for this attack technique?
A) Spear phishing
B) Baiting
C) Pretexting
D) Watering hole
Correct: B
Explanation: Baiting is a social engineering technique that uses physical objects (USB drives, CDs) left in accessible locations to exploit curiosity. Victims plug in the device, executing malware. This is a physical manifestation of the removable device threat vector combined with social engineering. Spear phishing and pretexting use direct communication. Watering hole attacks compromise websites.

Domain: 2.0 Threats, Vulnerabilities, and Mitigations
Sub-objective: 2.3 — Explain various types of vulnerabilities
Question: An attacker exploits a race condition in a setuid program to overwrite a sensitive system file between the program's permission check and its file write operation. Which exploit technique is this?
A) Buffer overflow
B) Privilege escalation via TOCTOU race condition
C) SQL injection
D) Memory injection
Correct: B
Explanation: Exploiting a Time-of-Check to Time-of-Use (TOCTOU) race condition in a privileged (setuid) program to overwrite system files achieves privilege escalation. The attacker wins the race by changing a symlink between the check and the write. This is a classic Linux privilege escalation technique. Buffer overflows corrupt memory directly. SQL injection targets databases. This is specifically a race condition exploited for privilege escalation.

Domain: 2.0 Threats, Vulnerabilities, and Mitigations
Sub-objective: 2.4 — Given a scenario, analyze indicators of malicious activity
Question: A user calls the help desk reporting that their screen shows a message claiming their computer is infected and instructing them to call a phone number immediately for help. Calling the number connects them to an attacker. Which attack type is this?
A) Ransomware with ransom demand
B) Scareware (fake alert) combined with vishing
C) Logic bomb activation
D) Trojan horse execution
Correct: B
Explanation: Fake security alerts designed to frighten users into calling attacker-controlled phone numbers combine scareware (false alarming messages) with vishing (voice phishing via the phone call). This tech support scam is common and does not typically involve actual malware — the psychological manipulation is the attack. Ransomware encrypts files. Logic bombs execute destructive code. Trojans hide malicious functionality in seemingly legitimate software.

Domain: 2.0 Threats, Vulnerabilities, and Mitigations
Sub-objective: 2.5 — Explain the purpose of mitigation techniques used to secure the enterprise
Question: A security policy mandates that all administrator accounts must use multi-factor authentication, and administrator privileges should be separate from day-to-day user accounts. Which mitigation principle does this implement?
A) Defense in depth through network segmentation
B) Least privilege and access control for privileged accounts
C) Configuration enforcement for endpoint security
D) Monitoring and alerting for privilege abuse
Correct: B
Explanation: Requiring separate admin accounts and MFA for privileged access implements least privilege (admin rights only in dedicated accounts, not used for daily activities) and access control (MFA gates access to privileged accounts). This limits the impact of credential compromise — a phished daily-use credential doesn't provide admin access. Network segmentation and monitoring are different mitigation categories.

Domain: 2.0 Threats, Vulnerabilities, and Mitigations
Sub-objective: 2.1 — Compare and contrast common threat actors and motivations
Question: Which statement BEST distinguishes organized crime from nation-state actors in the context of cyber threats?
A) Organized crime uses more sophisticated malware than nation-states
B) Organized crime primarily seeks financial gain, while nation-states pursue geopolitical objectives
C) Nation-states never conduct financially motivated attacks
D) Organized crime has greater resources and funding than nation-states
Correct: B
Explanation: The primary distinguishing factor is motivation: organized crime seeks profit (ransomware, fraud, data theft for sale) while nation-states pursue geopolitical goals (espionage, sabotage, influence operations). Nation-states do sometimes conduct financially motivated attacks (notably North Korea), but their primary driver is strategic national interest. Nation-states generally have greater resources, not organized crime.

Domain: 2.0 Threats, Vulnerabilities, and Mitigations
Sub-objective: 2.2 — Explain common threat vectors and attack surfaces
Question: An attacker intercepts and modifies network traffic between a client and server without either party detecting the change. Which attack type and threat vector does this represent?
A) Replay attack via removable media
B) On-path (man-in-the-middle) attack via unsecure network
C) Credential stuffing via message-based vector
D) Supply chain attack via vendor access
Correct: B
Explanation: Intercepting and modifying traffic in real time without detection is an on-path (man-in-the-middle) attack. This requires the attacker to be positioned between the communicating parties, typically by exploiting unsecure network infrastructure (ARP poisoning, rogue AP, BGP hijacking). This is different from a replay attack (which reuses previously captured data without modification). Removable media and supply chain are entirely different vectors.

Domain: 2.0 Threats, Vulnerabilities, and Mitigations
Sub-objective: 2.3 — Explain various types of vulnerabilities
Question: A development team deploys an application with verbose logging that records full credit card numbers in log files that are accessible to all developers. Which vulnerability category is this?
A) SQL injection
B) Cryptographic vulnerability
C) Misconfiguration leading to sensitive data exposure
D) Improper error handling
Correct: C
Explanation: Logging sensitive data (credit card numbers) to files accessible to unauthorized parties is a misconfiguration vulnerability — specifically a data handling misconfiguration. The system is not broken; it was configured to store data insecurely. This is different from improper error handling (which exposes data in error messages) and cryptographic vulnerabilities (which involve broken encryption). PCI DSS explicitly prohibits logging cardholder data.

Domain: 2.0 Threats, Vulnerabilities, and Mitigations
Sub-objective: 2.4 — Given a scenario, analyze indicators of malicious activity
Question: A security analyst investigates an alert showing that a workstation's hosts file has been modified to redirect "bank.com" to an attacker-controlled IP address. Which attack type is indicated?
A) DNS hijacking
B) Local hosts file poisoning (on-path attack setup)
C) Watering hole attack
D) Typosquatting
Correct: B
Explanation: Modifying the local hosts file to redirect legitimate domains to attacker-controlled IPs is a local hosts file poisoning attack — it achieves the same result as DNS hijacking but operates at the workstation level rather than the DNS infrastructure level. DNS hijacking occurs at the DNS server level. Watering hole attacks compromise legitimate websites. Typosquatting uses misspelled domain names rather than redirecting legitimate domain names.

Domain: 2.0 Threats, Vulnerabilities, and Mitigations
Sub-objective: 2.5 — Explain the purpose of mitigation techniques used to secure the enterprise
Question: An organization implements mandatory security awareness training covering phishing recognition, password security, and social engineering tactics. Which mitigation category does this represent?
A) Technical access control
B) Hardening
C) People-focused mitigation (security awareness)
D) Configuration enforcement
Correct: C
Explanation: Security awareness training is a people-focused (administrative/managerial) mitigation control targeting the human attack surface. Many attacks (phishing, social engineering, baiting) specifically target human behavior rather than technical vulnerabilities. Technical controls cannot fully compensate for uninformed users. Hardening applies to systems. Configuration enforcement applies to technical settings. Access control manages system permissions.

Domain: 2.0 Threats, Vulnerabilities, and Mitigations
Sub-objective: 2.3 — Explain various types of vulnerabilities
Question: A vulnerability scanner identifies an outdated version of OpenSSL with a known critical CVE in a production web server. The vendor released a patch 30 days ago. Which vulnerability type and contributing factor is present?
A) Zero-day vulnerability with no available patch
B) Known vulnerability unaddressed due to delayed patching
C) Cryptographic misconfiguration
D) Supply chain vulnerability
Correct: B
Explanation: A known CVE with an available patch that hasn't been applied is an unpatched known vulnerability — one of the most common and preventable security failures. This is distinct from a zero-day (which has no available patch). The contributing factor is the organization's patch management failure. The cryptographic library being vulnerable makes it a software vulnerability, not a cryptographic misconfiguration per se.

Domain: 2.0 Threats, Vulnerabilities, and Mitigations
Sub-objective: 2.4 — Given a scenario, analyze indicators of malicious activity
Question: A web application firewall alerts on requests containing "' OR 1=1 --" in login form fields. Which attack type is indicated, and what is the attacker attempting to achieve?
A) XSS attack attempting to inject a script
B) SQL injection attempting to bypass authentication
C) Buffer overflow attempting to crash the application
D) CSRF attempting to submit unauthorized requests
Correct: B
Explanation: "' OR 1=1 --" is a classic SQL injection payload attempting to modify a login query so it always returns true, bypassing authentication. The single quote breaks out of the string context, "OR 1=1" makes the condition always true, and "--" comments out the rest of the query. XSS uses script tags. Buffer overflows use excessively long input. CSRF forges cross-site requests rather than manipulating SQL.

Domain: 2.0 Threats, Vulnerabilities, and Mitigations
Sub-objective: 2.5 — Explain the purpose of mitigation techniques used to secure the enterprise
Question: A new employee is given access to only the file shares and applications required for their specific job role, reviewed quarterly. Which mitigation principle is being applied?
A) Configuration enforcement
B) Least privilege through periodic access reviews
C) Network segmentation
D) Application allow listing
Correct: B
Explanation: Granting only job-required access (least privilege) combined with periodic reviews to ensure access remains appropriate implements both least privilege and access control through ongoing validation. Access tends to accumulate over time ("privilege creep"), so quarterly reviews enforce the principle continuously. Configuration enforcement applies to system settings. Network segmentation controls network-level access, not application or file share permissions.

Domain: 2.0 Threats, Vulnerabilities, and Mitigations
Sub-objective: 2.2 — Explain common threat vectors and attack surfaces
Question: Attackers compromise a widely used open-source build tool and inject a backdoor into the build process that gets compiled into all software built with the tool. Which type of attack is this?
A) Watering hole attack
B) Build pipeline supply chain attack
C) Logic bomb activation
D) Zero-day exploitation
Correct: B
Explanation: Compromising the build pipeline (build tools, CI/CD systems, compilers) to inject malicious code into all software products built with the compromised tool is a build pipeline supply chain attack. The XZ Utils backdoor (CVE-2024-3094) is a real-world example. Watering hole attacks compromise websites. Logic bombs are pre-planted by insiders. Zero-days exploit previously unknown vulnerabilities.

Domain: 2.0 Threats, Vulnerabilities, and Mitigations
Sub-objective: 2.3 — Explain various types of vulnerabilities
Question: A penetration tester exploits a flaw in a hypervisor that allows a malicious virtual machine to read memory belonging to adjacent virtual machines on the same physical host. Which vulnerability type is this?
A) Side-channel attack
B) Resource reuse vulnerability
C) VM escape with memory access to co-located VMs
D) Cloud misconfiguration
Correct: C
Explanation: Escaping VM isolation to directly read another VM's memory represents VM escape — specifically the cross-VM memory access variant. Side-channel attacks infer information through indirect signals (timing, power) without breaking isolation. Resource reuse involves cleared-data remnants from previous use of shared storage, not active cross-VM memory access. Cloud misconfiguration is an administrative error, not a hypervisor flaw.

Domain: 2.0 Threats, Vulnerabilities, and Mitigations
Sub-objective: 2.4 — Given a scenario, analyze indicators of malicious activity
Question: An organization's SIEM correlates events showing: (1) a user opened a phishing email attachment, (2) PowerShell downloaded a second-stage payload, (3) the process established persistence via registry run key, (4) it began scanning internal hosts. Which attack phase sequence is this?
A) Lateral movement → persistence → reconnaissance → initial access
B) Initial access → execution → persistence → lateral movement/reconnaissance
C) Persistence → execution → lateral movement → initial access
D) Reconnaissance → initial access → persistence → execution
Correct: B
Explanation: This sequence maps to the MITRE ATT&CK framework: Initial Access (phishing email), Execution (PowerShell downloading payload), Persistence (registry run key), then Reconnaissance/Lateral Movement (scanning internal hosts). Understanding kill chain/attack sequence order is important for Security+. This is the standard post-compromise attack progression. Recognizing this sequence helps analysts prioritize containment actions.

Domain: 2.0 Threats, Vulnerabilities, and Mitigations
Sub-objective: 2.5 — Explain the purpose of mitigation techniques used to secure the enterprise
Question: A security team implements network access control (NAC) that checks endpoint health (patch status, AV signature currency, approved configuration) before allowing network access. Which mitigation approach does this represent?
A) Network segmentation only
B) Configuration enforcement combined with access control
C) Application allow listing
D) Monitoring and alerting
Correct: B
Explanation: NAC enforces configuration requirements (patch status, AV currency) as a condition of network access — this combines configuration enforcement (verifying the endpoint meets security standards) with access control (blocking non-compliant endpoints). It is not just segmentation (though NAC can place endpoints in quarantine VLANs). It goes beyond monitoring by actively blocking non-compliant devices.

Domain: 2.0 Threats, Vulnerabilities, and Mitigations
Sub-objective: 2.1 — Compare and contrast common threat actors and motivations
Question: Which motivational factor is MOST likely to drive a foreign nation-state to target a pharmaceutical company's research networks?
A) Financial extortion through ransomware
B) Ideological opposition to the company's business practices
C) Economic espionage to gain competitive advantage without R&D investment
D) Service disruption to harm the company's market position
Correct: C
Explanation: Nation-states targeting pharmaceutical research are motivated by economic espionage — stealing R&D results to benefit domestic industries without the investment cost. This is particularly common for vaccine research, drug development, and medical technology. Ransomware extortion is organized crime's domain. Ideological opposition is hacktivist motivation. Service disruption is a short-term tactical goal, not a strategic espionage objective.

Domain: 2.0 Threats, Vulnerabilities, and Mitigations
Sub-objective: 2.2 — Explain common threat vectors and attack surfaces
Question: A security analyst discovers that employees are regularly connecting to the corporate network via public hotel Wi-Fi without using a VPN. Which specific risks does this create?
A) Risk of logic bomb deployment via the network
B) On-path attack and credential interception via unsecure wireless network
C) Supply chain compromise through third-party network providers
D) RFID cloning attacks from nearby attackers
Correct: B
Explanation: Connecting to uncontrolled public Wi-Fi without VPN creates on-path (MITM) attack risk — an attacker on the same network can intercept unencrypted traffic and capture credentials. This is an unsecure wireless network attack vector. VPN creates an encrypted tunnel that protects against this. Logic bombs require planted code. Supply chain attacks target the development pipeline. RFID cloning is a separate physical attack.

Domain: 2.0 Threats, Vulnerabilities, and Mitigations
Sub-objective: 2.3 — Explain various types of vulnerabilities
Question: A web application uses client-side JavaScript to validate input before submitting to the server, but performs no server-side validation. An attacker bypasses the client-side checks by modifying the HTTP request directly. Which vulnerability exists?
A) Cross-Site Scripting
B) Reliance on client-side validation — an application vulnerability
C) CSRF vulnerability
D) Buffer overflow
Correct: B
Explanation: Relying solely on client-side validation is an application vulnerability because client-side controls can always be bypassed (through browser developer tools, proxy tools like Burp Suite, or direct HTTP requests). All security-relevant input validation must occur server-side. This is a fundamental application security principle. XSS and CSRF are different web vulnerabilities. Buffer overflows are memory corruption issues.

Domain: 2.0 Threats, Vulnerabilities, and Mitigations
Sub-objective: 2.4 — Given a scenario, analyze indicators of malicious activity
Question: A threat hunter reviews endpoint telemetry and finds a process "svchost.exe" running from the path "C:\Users\Public\svchost.exe" rather than "C:\Windows\System32\svchost.exe". Which malicious activity indicator is this?
A) Process running from an unexpected location (masquerading)
B) Privilege escalation indicator
C) Logic bomb activation indicator
D) Rootkit hidden process indicator
Correct: A
Explanation: A legitimate Windows process name running from an unusual path (C:\Users\Public\ instead of C:\Windows\System32\) is a classic masquerading technique — malware named to look like a legitimate process but running from a user-writable location. This is a key threat hunting indicator. Detecting process path anomalies is an important behavioral detection technique. Rootkits typically hide processes entirely rather than showing them with unusual paths.

Domain: 2.0 Threats, Vulnerabilities, and Mitigations
Sub-objective: 2.5 — Explain the purpose of mitigation techniques used to secure the enterprise
Question: After discovering that an attacker maintained access to a compromised server for six months through a web shell, the security team implements file integrity monitoring on all web directories. Which mitigation capability does this implement?
A) Preventive control — prevents web shell installation
B) Corrective control — removes existing web shells
C) Detective control — alerts on unauthorized file changes
D) Recovery control — restores files after deletion
Correct: C
Explanation: File integrity monitoring (FIM) is a detective control — it monitors files for unauthorized changes and alerts security teams. It would have detected the web shell installation rather than preventing it. FIM does not prevent or remove web shells — it detects them by comparing file hashes or monitoring for changes. This enables faster incident detection and response. Preventive controls (WAF, access controls) would restrict the ability to write web shells.

Domain: 2.0 Threats, Vulnerabilities, and Mitigations
Sub-objective: 2.4 — Given a scenario, analyze indicators of malicious activity
Question: Network flow data shows a workstation sending 2GB of data to an external cloud storage service over three hours during business hours. Normal outbound transfers for this workstation average 50MB per day. Which indicator category does this represent?
A) Indicator of network scanning activity
B) Indicator of data exfiltration
C) Indicator of DDoS participation
D) Indicator of credential stuffing
Correct: B
Explanation: A 40x increase in outbound data transfer to an external cloud storage service far outside the normal baseline is a behavioral indicator of data exfiltration. The pattern (large volume, external destination, cloud storage service) is consistent with an insider threat or malware copying sensitive data. Network scanning would show many destination IPs. DDoS would show traffic to a single external IP with high packet rates.

Domain: 2.0 Threats, Vulnerabilities, and Mitigations
Sub-objective: 2.3 — Explain various types of vulnerabilities
Question: A security researcher demonstrates that an attacker who can execute code inside a container can read the host filesystem by exploiting a kernel vulnerability shared between the container and host. Which vulnerability type is demonstrated?
A) VM escape
B) Resource reuse
C) Container escape via OS vulnerability
D) Side-channel attack
Correct: C
Explanation: Container escape exploiting a shared kernel vulnerability is analogous to VM escape but specific to containers. Containers share the host OS kernel (unlike VMs which have separate kernels), meaning kernel vulnerabilities can allow container breakout. This falls under virtualization vulnerabilities. VM escape specifically refers to hypervisor-based virtualization. Resource reuse involves data remnants. Side-channel attacks use indirect information leakage.

Domain: 2.0 Threats, Vulnerabilities, and Mitigations
Sub-objective: 2.2 — Explain common threat vectors and attack surfaces
Question: An attacker gains access to a large enterprise by first compromising a small HVAC maintenance contractor that has VPN access to the target's building management systems. This entry method is an example of which attack vector?
A) Watering hole attack
B) Supply chain / trusted third-party attack vector
C) Default credential exploitation
D) Removable device vector
Correct: B
Explanation: Compromising a trusted third-party vendor with legitimate network access to gain entry into the primary target is a supply chain / trusted third-party attack — notably used in the famous Target data breach. The attacker exploits the trust relationship and access granted to the vendor. Watering hole attacks compromise websites. Default credentials and removable devices are different vectors not involving third-party vendor access.

Domain: 2.0 Threats, Vulnerabilities, and Mitigations
Sub-objective: 2.5 — Explain the purpose of mitigation techniques used to secure the enterprise
Question: A security architect proposes replacing flat network architecture with tiered zones, implementing zero-trust network access, and requiring authentication for all east-west traffic within the data center. Which mitigation strategy encompasses all of these?
A) Comprehensive hardening of individual endpoints
B) Defense in depth through layered network access controls and segmentation
C) Application allow listing across all workloads
D) Monitoring and SIEM implementation
Correct: B
Explanation: Tiered network zones, zero-trust (authenticate all traffic), and east-west traffic controls together form a defense-in-depth approach using layered network segmentation and access controls. Zero-trust network access (ZTNA) assumes breach and requires authentication for all internal traffic, limiting lateral movement. This is an architectural/network control strategy. Endpoint hardening, allow listing, and SIEM are different mitigation categories.

Domain: 2.0 Threats, Vulnerabilities, and Mitigations
Sub-objective: 2.1 — Compare and contrast common threat actors and motivations
Question: A security operations team discovers that their company's internal source code repository has been accessed by an unknown external party over several weeks. Investigation reveals the attacker used valid credentials belonging to a contractor. The contractor reports having no knowledge of the access. Which threat actor scenario BEST fits?
A) Disgruntled insider conducting espionage
B) Script kiddie using automated credential stuffing
C) APT using stolen contractor credentials for persistent access
D) Hacktivist targeting the company for ideological reasons
Correct: C
Explanation: Sustained access over weeks using stolen credentials targeting source code is consistent with an APT (nation-state or sophisticated organized crime) using credential theft to maintain covert access. APTs frequently compromise third-party credentials to gain access while minimizing their own footprint. Script kiddies lack the patience and operational sophistication for weeks-long covert access. Hacktivists typically publicize their attacks rather than remaining covert.

Domain: 2.0 Threats, Vulnerabilities, and Mitigations
Sub-objective: 2.4 — Given a scenario, analyze indicators of malicious activity
Question: During incident response, an analyst finds that a malware sample creates a scheduled task named "WindowsUpdateService", establishes a connection to an IP on port 443, sends small amounts of data every 60 seconds, and accepts commands from the remote server. Which malicious behavior is described?
A) Ransomware encrypting files and contacting payment infrastructure
B) Worm scanning for vulnerable systems to propagate
C) Remote access trojan (RAT) with C2 beaconing and persistence
D) Spyware sending keystrokes to an attacker
Correct: C
Explanation: Regular small data transmissions on port 443 (HTTPS — blends with legitimate traffic) at fixed intervals (beaconing), a persistence mechanism (scheduled task with a legitimately-sounding name), and the ability to receive commands describes a Remote Access Trojan (RAT) with Command and Control (C2) beaconing. RATs provide attackers with remote control capabilities. Ransomware encrypts files. Worms spread autonomously. Spyware sends captured data but doesn't typically provide two-way command execution.

Domain: 2.0 Threats, Vulnerabilities, and Mitigations
Sub-objective: 2.5 — Explain the purpose of mitigation techniques used to secure the enterprise
Question: A financial institution implements separation of duties requiring that the person who creates a wire transfer cannot also approve it. Which security principle is enforced and what threat does it mitigate?
A) Least privilege — prevents privilege escalation attacks
B) Separation of duties — mitigates insider fraud and error
C) Configuration enforcement — prevents misconfiguration
D) Isolation — prevents lateral movement
Correct: B
Explanation: Separation of duties divides sensitive processes across multiple individuals so no single person can complete a fraudulent transaction alone. In financial processes, requiring different people for creation and approval is the classic separation of duties implementation. It mitigates insider fraud (collusion required) and unintentional error (second set of eyes). Least privilege limits what each role can do. Isolation and configuration enforcement are different control types.

Domain: 2.0 Threats, Vulnerabilities, and Mitigations
Sub-objective: 2.3 — Explain various types of vulnerabilities
Question: A penetration tester discovers that when the tested application encounters an exception, it falls back to a less-secure code path that grants elevated access rather than failing safely. Which vulnerability type is this?
A) Privilege escalation via improper error handling
B) Buffer overflow leading to privilege escalation
C) Race condition in privilege checking
D) SQL injection enabling privilege escalation
Correct: A
Explanation: An application that grants elevated access when it encounters an error rather than denying access and failing safely demonstrates improper error handling. The principle of "fail secure" or "fail closed" requires that errors result in denial of access, not elevation. This is a distinct application vulnerability where exception handling logic creates a privilege escalation path. Buffer overflows and race conditions are different memory/timing vulnerability categories.

Domain: 2.0 Threats, Vulnerabilities, and Mitigations
Sub-objective: 2.4 — Given a scenario, analyze indicators of malicious activity
Question: Security researchers analyze a malware sample and determine it actively looks for antivirus processes and terminates them, uses encryption to hide C2 communications, and employs code obfuscation to evade signature detection. These are indicators of which malware sophistication level and type?
A) Script kiddie-level adware
B) Bloatware with annoying but benign behavior
C) Sophisticated evasion-capable malware (likely nation-state or organized crime)
D) Simple keylogger without evasion capabilities
Correct: C
Explanation: AV termination (defense evasion), encrypted C2 (detection evasion), and code obfuscation (signature evasion) are hallmarks of sophisticated, professionally developed malware associated with nation-state or advanced organized crime threat actors. These techniques require significant development expertise. Script kiddie tools don't incorporate active defense evasion. Bloatware and simple keyloggers lack these advanced capabilities.

Domain: 2.0 Threats, Vulnerabilities, and Mitigations
Sub-objective: 2.2 — Explain common threat vectors and attack surfaces
Question: A user reports receiving a voicemail from "IRS Collections" demanding immediate payment of back taxes via gift cards or face arrest. This is an example of which attack type?
A) Spear phishing via email
B) Vishing (voice phishing) using authority and urgency
C) Smishing via voicemail
D) Pretexting via watering hole
Correct: B
Explanation: A phone-based social engineering attack impersonating an authority figure (IRS) and using urgency (arrest threat) to manipulate the victim into immediate action is vishing. The psychological manipulation elements — authority (IRS), urgency (arrest), and an unusual payment method (gift cards) — are classic vishing indicators. This is voice-based (vishing), not SMS-based (smishing) or email-based (phishing). The voicemail delivery is still a voice channel.

Domain: 2.0 Threats, Vulnerabilities, and Mitigations
Sub-objective: 2.3 — Explain various types of vulnerabilities
Question: An application uses MD5 to hash passwords stored in a database. A data breach exposes the hashed passwords, and attackers crack them using precomputed rainbow tables within hours. Which vulnerability is present?
A) SQL injection enabling database access
B) Cryptographic vulnerability — use of weak, broken hash algorithm
C) Misconfiguration — passwords not salted
D) Improper error handling
Correct: B
Explanation: MD5 is a cryptographically broken algorithm — it is fast (enabling rapid brute force) and has known collision vulnerabilities. Using MD5 for password hashing is a cryptographic vulnerability. While the lack of salting is also a contributing misconfiguration, the root issue is using a broken algorithm. Password hashing requires slow, purpose-built algorithms (bcrypt, Argon2, scrypt) that resist GPU-accelerated attacks. The question specifically tests recognition of weak cryptographic algorithms as a vulnerability category.

Domain: 2.0 Threats, Vulnerabilities, and Mitigations
Sub-objective: 2.4 — Given a scenario, analyze indicators of malicious activity
Question: A threat hunter finds that a process on a server is making regular HTTPS connections to a domain registered six days ago with WHOIS privacy protection. The domain name is a random-looking 16-character string. Which malicious activity indicator is this?
A) Indicator of brute force authentication attempts
B) Indicator of domain generation algorithm (DGA) C2 communication
C) Indicator of DNS tunneling
D) Indicator of watering hole attack staging
Correct: B
Explanation: Connections to recently registered domains with random-looking names are indicators of Domain Generation Algorithm (DGA) based malware — many botnet families generate pseudorandom domain names that beaconing malware tries to contact until it reaches an active C2 server. The short registration age and random-looking name are the key indicators. DNS tunneling uses high volumes of DNS queries, not HTTPS connections. Brute force involves authentication attempts.

Domain: 2.0 Threats, Vulnerabilities, and Mitigations
Sub-objective: 2.5 — Explain the purpose of mitigation techniques used to secure the enterprise
Question: An organization requires all web traffic from endpoints to pass through a cloud-based secure web gateway that inspects and blocks malicious content, regardless of whether the user is on-premises or remote. Which mitigation technique does this represent?
A) Network segmentation between internal zones
B) Isolation of compromised endpoints
C) Monitoring through traffic inspection and filtering
D) Configuration enforcement via proxy policy
Correct: C
Explanation: A secure web gateway that inspects and blocks malicious web traffic implements monitoring (inspection) combined with filtering/blocking (access control). The inspection capability provides visibility into web traffic. The blocking capability prevents access to malicious sites. This addresses web-based threat vectors. Network segmentation applies to internal zone separation. Isolation is a reactive containment technique. Configuration enforcement applies to endpoint settings.

Domain: 2.0 Threats, Vulnerabilities, and Mitigations
Sub-objective: 2.1 — Compare and contrast common threat actors and motivations
Question: A threat intelligence report describes an actor that conducts attacks for both financial gain and ideological reasons, targeting companies that conduct animal testing. The group releases stolen research data publicly rather than selling it. Which threat actor type BEST describes this group?
A) Organized crime with opportunistic targeting
B) Nation-state conducting economic espionage
C) Hacktivist with financial sustainability concerns
D) Insider threat group
Correct: C
Explanation: Targeting companies for ideological reasons (opposition to animal testing) and releasing stolen data publicly rather than monetizing it is classic hacktivist behavior. The financial component may fund operations rather than being the primary motivation. Publishing stolen data to create reputational harm and public awareness is a hacktivist tactic. Nation-states keep stolen data secret. Organized crime monetizes data. Insiders require legitimate access.

Domain: 2.0 Threats, Vulnerabilities, and Mitigations
Sub-objective: 2.2 — Explain common threat vectors and attack surfaces
Question: A security team discovers that a widely used third-party analytics JavaScript library included in their website has been modified to include a credit card skimmer. This type of attack has a specific name. Which attack is this?
A) Watering hole attack
B) Formjacking / web skimming supply chain attack
C) Cross-Site Scripting
D) Typosquatting
Correct: B
Explanation: Compromising a third-party JavaScript library to inject payment card skimming code into websites that include the library is called formjacking or web skimming — a supply chain attack targeting the software supply chain. Magecart is the most famous threat actor group conducting these attacks. This differs from XSS (which requires a vulnerability in the target site) because the malicious code is introduced through a trusted third-party resource.

Domain: 2.0 Threats, Vulnerabilities, and Mitigations
Sub-objective: 2.3 — Explain various types of vulnerabilities
Question: A security researcher discovers a flaw in a popular CPU microarchitecture that allows a process to speculatively read memory it shouldn't have access to, potentially leaking data across security boundaries. This class of vulnerability is known as:
A) Virtual machine escape
B) Buffer overflow vulnerability
C) Speculative execution / microarchitectural side-channel vulnerability
D) Race condition vulnerability
Correct: C
Explanation: CPU speculative execution vulnerabilities (Spectre, Meltdown) exploit microarchitectural features (speculative execution and cache timing) to leak data across security boundaries. These are microarchitectural side-channel vulnerabilities. They affect hardware at a fundamental level rather than software. VM escape requires software-level hypervisor flaws. Buffer overflows corrupt memory through software. Race conditions involve timing in software execution.

Domain: 2.0 Threats, Vulnerabilities, and Mitigations
Sub-objective: 2.4 — Given a scenario, analyze indicators of malicious activity
Question: An attacker gains physical access to a building by following an authorized employee through a secured door without badging in. Which type of physical attack is this?
A) RFID cloning
B) Tailgating / piggybacking
C) Environmental attack
D) Dumpster diving
Correct: B
Explanation: Tailgating (also called piggybacking) involves following an authorized person through a secured entrance without using one's own credentials. It exploits social norms (holding doors open) to bypass physical access controls. RFID cloning involves copying access card data to create a duplicate. Environmental attacks target physical infrastructure (HVAC, power). Dumpster diving involves searching discarded materials for information.

Domain: 2.0 Threats, Vulnerabilities, and Mitigations
Sub-objective: 2.5 — Explain the purpose of mitigation techniques used to secure the enterprise
Question: A security team implements automatic revocation of access rights for employees immediately upon HR system notification of termination, ensuring accounts are disabled before the final workday ends. Which mitigation principle does this implement?
A) Monitoring and alerting
B) Configuration enforcement
C) Access control through prompt deprovisioning
D) Least privilege review
Correct: C
Explanation: Prompt deprovisioning of access upon employee termination is a critical access control process that prevents former employees from retaining unauthorized access. This directly mitigates the insider threat risk from disgruntled former employees. It is an access control lifecycle management technique. Monitoring detects unauthorized access after the fact. Configuration enforcement manages system settings. Least privilege reviews adjust existing access levels.

Domain: 2.0 Threats, Vulnerabilities, and Mitigations
Sub-objective: 2.3 — Explain various types of vulnerabilities
Question: A developer inadvertently commits AWS access keys to a public GitHub repository. An automated bot discovers the keys within minutes and begins spinning up cryptocurrency mining instances. Which vulnerability category allowed this breach?
A) Supply chain vulnerability
B) Cryptographic vulnerability
C) Misconfiguration — insecure credential storage
D) Zero-day vulnerability
Correct: C
Explanation: Committing credentials (API keys, passwords) to version control is a misconfiguration and insecure credential storage vulnerability. Credentials should never be hardcoded in source code. This is one of the most common cloud security failures. It is not a supply chain vulnerability (which involves compromised components), not a cryptographic vulnerability (the credentials themselves were exposed, not broken cryptography), and not a zero-day.

Domain: 2.0 Threats, Vulnerabilities, and Mitigations
Sub-objective: 2.4 — Given a scenario, analyze indicators of malicious activity
Question: Analysis of a malware sample shows it spreads by attaching a copy of itself to executable files on the infected system and any connected network shares. Each time an infected file is executed, the malware activates and spreads further. Which malware type is this?
A) Worm
B) Trojan horse
C) Virus
D) Rootkit
Correct: C
Explanation: A virus attaches copies of itself to existing executable files and requires a host file to spread. When infected files are executed, the virus activates. This self-replication via file infection is the defining characteristic distinguishing viruses from worms (which spread independently without needing host files) and trojans (which don't self-replicate). Rootkits provide hidden persistent access but don't typically spread by infecting files.

Domain: 2.0 Threats, Vulnerabilities, and Mitigations
Sub-objective: 2.5 — Explain the purpose of mitigation techniques used to secure the enterprise
Question: A company replaces all end-of-life network switches and firewalls that can no longer receive security firmware updates, even though the equipment still functions operationally. Which mitigation principle drives this decision?
A) Configuration enforcement
B) Monitoring expansion
C) Decommissioning to eliminate unmitigable vulnerabilities
D) Network segmentation redesign
Correct: C
Explanation: Replacing end-of-life equipment that can no longer receive security patches is a decommissioning decision to eliminate vulnerabilities that cannot be mitigated through patching. EOL hardware with unpatchable vulnerabilities represents permanent, growing risk. While expensive, decommissioning is sometimes the only mitigation option. Configuration enforcement cannot fix firmware vulnerabilities. Monitoring detects but doesn't prevent exploitation.

Domain: 2.0 Threats, Vulnerabilities, and Mitigations
Sub-objective: 2.4 — Given a scenario, analyze indicators of malicious activity
Question: A security analyst reviews proxy logs and notices a workstation making GET requests to a URL containing a long Base64-encoded string in the URL path at regular intervals, with responses containing small amounts of encoded data. Which malicious activity is indicated?
A) DDoS attack participation
B) Ransomware contacting its payment infrastructure
C) HTTP-based C2 communication with encoded data
D) Credential stuffing against a web application
Correct: C
Explanation: Regular HTTP/HTTPS GET requests with Base64-encoded data in URL paths at fixed intervals, with encoded responses, is a pattern of HTTP-based Command and Control (C2) communication. Malware uses HTTP/HTTPS to blend with normal web traffic, and encodes data to obscure its content. The regular interval (beaconing) is a key indicator. Credential stuffing shows many POST requests to authentication endpoints. DDoS participation shows high-volume traffic to a single external destination.

Domain: 2.0 Threats, Vulnerabilities, and Mitigations
Sub-objective: 2.2 — Explain common threat vectors and attack surfaces
Question: A company's security policy prohibits the use of Bluetooth on corporate laptops due to attack surface concerns. Which specific Bluetooth attack type involves an attacker forcing unauthorized connections to a Bluetooth-enabled device to exfiltrate data?
A) Bluejacking
B) Bluesnarfing
C) Evil twin via Bluetooth
D) Bluetooth relay attack
Correct: B
Explanation: Bluesnarfing involves an attacker exploiting Bluetooth vulnerabilities to gain unauthorized access to a device and exfiltrate data (contacts, messages, calendar data). Bluejacking is less harmful — it involves sending unsolicited messages to nearby Bluetooth devices. Evil twin attacks typically apply to Wi-Fi. Relay attacks are used against proximity card systems. The SY0-701 tests awareness of specific Bluetooth attack types.

Domain: 2.0 Threats, Vulnerabilities, and Mitigations
Sub-objective: 2.3 — Explain various types of vulnerabilities
Question: A security team discovers that a critical healthcare application still runs on Windows Server 2008 R2 because updating it would require re-certifying the application for regulatory compliance, a two-year process. Which vulnerability category is exemplified, and why is it particularly risky?
A) Zero-day vulnerability — exploitable before patches exist
B) Legacy/end-of-life OS vulnerability — no security patches available against known CVEs
C) Misconfiguration — the server is not properly configured
D) Supply chain vulnerability — the vendor is no longer providing support
Correct: B
Explanation: Windows Server 2008 R2 reached end-of-life in January 2020, meaning Microsoft no longer provides security patches. New CVEs discovered after EOL will never be patched, creating permanently unaddressable vulnerabilities. Healthcare and industrial environments commonly face this challenge due to certification requirements. This is the legacy/EOL OS vulnerability category. Zero-days affect supported software with no patches yet available. Misconfiguration is a different category.

Domain: 2.0 Threats, Vulnerabilities, and Mitigations
Sub-objective: 2.4 — Given a scenario, analyze indicators of malicious activity
Question: An organization detects that an attacker accessed several systems in sequence: initially the HR workstation, then the HR manager's laptop, then the Active Directory server, then the file server. Which attack technique is this progression an indicator of?
A) Vertical privilege escalation
B) Lateral movement
C) Defense evasion
D) Initial access
Correct: B
Explanation: Sequential access to multiple hosts within an environment — moving from one compromised system to another to reach higher-value targets — is lateral movement. The attacker is using each compromised system as a staging point to reach the next target. This is distinct from vertical privilege escalation (gaining higher privileges on the same system). Recognizing lateral movement patterns is essential for incident response containment.

Domain: 2.0 Threats, Vulnerabilities, and Mitigations
Sub-objective: 2.5 — Explain the purpose of mitigation techniques used to secure the enterprise
Question: To prevent an attacker who compromises a web server from accessing the internal database server, a security architect implements a rule that only the web server's IP address can connect to the database server on port 1433. Which mitigation technique is this?
A) Application allow listing
B) Network access control via segmentation and firewall rules
C) Encryption of database connections
D) Least privilege for database accounts
Correct: B
Explanation: Restricting database server access to only the web server IP via firewall rules implements network segmentation with access control. Even if the web server is compromised, the attacker is constrained by the firewall rule — they can access the database only through the web server's existing connection, not establish new direct connections. Application allow listing controls software execution. Database encryption protects data at rest/in transit. Least privilege controls database account permissions.

Domain: 2.0 Threats, Vulnerabilities, and Mitigations
Sub-objective: 2.1 — Compare and contrast common threat actors and motivations
Question: An employee who was recently laid off accesses the company's customer database using credentials that were not revoked and exfiltrates customer data to sell to a competitor. Which threat actor type and motivation combination is MOST accurate?
A) External actor, financial motivation
B) Former insider threat, financial motivation
C) Hacktivist, ideological motivation
D) Nation-state actor, espionage motivation
Correct: B
Explanation: A recently laid-off employee retains insider-level knowledge and potentially still-valid credentials, making them a former insider threat. The motivation is financial (selling data to a competitor). This scenario highlights both the importance of prompt credential revocation (deprovisioning) and the insider threat risk. External actors don't have legitimate credentials. Hacktivists and nation-states have different motivations.

Domain: 2.0 Threats, Vulnerabilities, and Mitigations
Sub-objective: 2.3 — Explain various types of vulnerabilities
Question: A red team discovers that a cloud environment has IAM policies granting every EC2 instance full administrative access to all S3 buckets in the account, even though only two instances actually need this access. Which vulnerability category is this?
A) Zero-day vulnerability
B) Side-channel vulnerability
C) Cloud misconfiguration — overly permissive IAM policy
D) Cryptographic vulnerability
Correct: C
Explanation: Overly permissive IAM policies in cloud environments are a misconfiguration vulnerability and one of the most common cloud security failures. Granting all instances full admin access to all S3 buckets violates least privilege and means a single compromised instance can access all data. This is a configuration error (the technology works correctly; it's been configured insecurely). Zero-days, side-channels, and cryptographic issues are fundamentally different vulnerability types.

Domain: 2.0 Threats, Vulnerabilities, and Mitigations
Sub-objective: 2.4 — Given a scenario, analyze indicators of malicious activity
Question: During a forensic investigation, an analyst discovers that log files on a compromised server covering the period of the intrusion have been deleted. The attacker also modified timestamps on remaining files. Which malicious activity category do these actions fall under?
A) Data exfiltration indicators
B) Lateral movement indicators
C) Anti-forensics and evidence tampering
D) Ransomware staging indicators
Correct: C
Explanation: Deleting log files and modifying file timestamps to eliminate or obscure evidence of malicious activity are anti-forensics techniques — actions taken to hinder incident response and forensic investigation. This falls under the defense evasion tactic in MITRE ATT&CK. Data exfiltration involves sending data externally. Lateral movement involves moving between hosts. Ransomware staging involves preparing for encryption.

Domain: 2.0 Threats, Vulnerabilities, and Mitigations
Sub-objective: 2.5 — Explain the purpose of mitigation techniques used to secure the enterprise
Question: A security policy mandates that all laptops have full-disk encryption enabled using a platform-managed key stored in the device's TPM, unlocked only after successful authentication. Which threats does this mitigation PRIMARILY address?
A) Network-based attacks against the laptop
B) Malware execution on the operating system
C) Data theft from stolen or lost physical devices
D) Unauthorized remote access to the laptop
Correct: C
Explanation: Full-disk encryption with TPM-backed key management protects against physical theft — if a laptop is stolen, the data on the drive cannot be read without authenticating to the device. This is a confidentiality control for the physical threat vector. It does not protect against network attacks, prevent malware from running on a booted, authenticated system, or protect against remote access attacks. It specifically addresses the risk of data exposure from physical device loss.
Domain: 3.0 Security Architecture
Sub-objective: 3.1 — Compare and contrast security implications of different architecture models
Question: Which cloud service model places the MOST security responsibility on the cloud provider?
A) IaaS
B) PaaS
C) SaaS
D) Hybrid cloud
Correct: C
Explanation: In SaaS, the cloud provider manages the application, runtime, OS, storage, and infrastructure, leaving the customer responsible only for data and user access. In IaaS, the customer manages everything from the OS upward. Hybrid cloud is a deployment model, not a service model.

Domain: 3.0 Security Architecture
Sub-objective: 3.1 — Compare and contrast security implications of different architecture models
Question: A company hosts its own servers in a dedicated facility that is not shared with other organisations. Which cloud deployment model does this describe?
A) Public cloud
B) Community cloud
C) Hybrid cloud
D) Private cloud
Correct: D
Explanation: A private cloud is dedicated exclusively to one organisation and can be hosted on-premises or in a third-party facility. A public cloud shares infrastructure across many tenants. A community cloud is shared among organisations with common interests. Hybrid cloud combines private and public models.

Domain: 3.0 Security Architecture
Sub-objective: 3.1 — Compare and contrast security implications of different architecture models
Question: In the shared responsibility model for IaaS, which of the following is the customer's responsibility?
A) Physical security of the data centre
B) Hypervisor patching
C) Guest operating system patching
D) Network cable plant maintenance
Correct: C
Explanation: In IaaS, the provider manages physical infrastructure, hardware, and the hypervisor. The customer is responsible for the guest OS, applications, and data running on the virtual machines. Physical security and network hardware are always the provider's responsibility in IaaS.

Domain: 3.0 Security Architecture
Sub-objective: 3.1 — Compare and contrast security implications of different architecture models
Question: Which architecture model uses templates and code to provision and manage infrastructure consistently and repeatably?
A) Containerization
B) Infrastructure as Code (IaC)
C) Serverless computing
D) Software-defined networking
Correct: B
Explanation: Infrastructure as Code (IaC) uses declarative or imperative definition files (e.g., Terraform, CloudFormation) to provision infrastructure consistently. A key security benefit is version control and auditability of infrastructure changes. Containerization packages applications; serverless abstracts server management; SDN virtualises network control.

Domain: 3.0 Security Architecture
Sub-objective: 3.1 — Compare and contrast security implications of different architecture models
Question: A security risk unique to Infrastructure as Code environments is that a single misconfigured template can be:
A) Rolled back automatically by the hypervisor
B) Deployed identically across hundreds of systems simultaneously
C) Detected by endpoint detection and response tools
D) Isolated to a single availability zone
Correct: B
Explanation: IaC allows rapid, consistent deployment — but this also means a security misconfiguration (e.g., open S3 bucket policy, missing encryption setting) is replicated at scale before it can be caught. This amplification risk is the primary security concern with IaC templates. EDR tools operate at the endpoint level, not at the IaC deployment level.

Domain: 3.0 Security Architecture
Sub-objective: 3.1 — Compare and contrast security implications of different architecture models
Question: Which of the following BEST describes a serverless architecture from a security perspective?
A) The customer manages the underlying OS and runtime patches
B) The provider abstracts server management, shifting runtime security responsibility to the provider
C) All compute nodes are owned and operated by the customer
D) Network segmentation is not required because functions are stateless
Correct: B
Explanation: In serverless (e.g., AWS Lambda, Azure Functions), the provider manages all infrastructure including the OS and runtime. The customer is responsible for the function code and its logic, including input validation and secrets management. Network segmentation may still apply depending on how functions interact with VPCs and other resources.

Domain: 3.0 Security Architecture
Sub-objective: 3.1 — Compare and contrast security implications of different architecture models
Question: A microservices architecture improves security isolation primarily because:
A) Each service shares a common database schema, reducing attack surface
B) Services communicate only through encrypted hardware channels
C) Compromise of one service does not automatically grant access to all other services
D) Microservices eliminate the need for API gateways
Correct: C
Explanation: Microservices decompose applications into small, independently deployable services. This limits the blast radius of a breach — an attacker who compromises one service must separately attack others. The tempting distractor is the shared database model; in practice, microservices should use separate data stores to maintain this isolation.

Domain: 3.0 Security Architecture
Sub-objective: 3.1 — Compare and contrast security implications of different architecture models
Question: Containers differ from virtual machines in that containers:
A) Provide stronger hardware-level isolation between workloads
B) Share the host OS kernel, making kernel vulnerabilities a shared risk
C) Cannot be deployed on public cloud infrastructure
D) Always include a full OS image in each container
Correct: B
Explanation: Containers share the host OS kernel, so a kernel exploit can potentially affect all containers on that host. VMs have their own kernel within a guest OS, providing stronger isolation. This shared kernel model is the key security distinction. Containers do include application dependencies but not a full OS.

Domain: 3.0 Security Architecture
Sub-objective: 3.1 — Compare and contrast security implications of different architecture models
Question: A hospital system uses a cloud environment shared with several other healthcare organisations to meet HIPAA requirements collectively. This is an example of which deployment model?
A) Hybrid cloud
B) Community cloud
C) Public cloud
D) Private cloud
Correct: B
Explanation: A community cloud is shared by organisations with common regulatory or operational concerns — in this case, multiple healthcare organisations sharing HIPAA compliance obligations. A hybrid cloud combines private and public resources. A public cloud is open to any tenant without regard to regulatory alignment.

Domain: 3.0 Security Architecture
Sub-objective: 3.1 — Compare and contrast security implications of different architecture models
Question: Which of the following is a PRIMARY security concern when an organisation uses third-party cloud vendors?
A) Inability to use encryption for data at rest
B) Loss of direct visibility and control over security controls applied to data
C) Automatic elevation of the organisation's attack surface to nation-state levels
D) Prohibition of multi-factor authentication by the vendor
Correct: B
Explanation: When relying on third-party vendors, an organisation must trust that the vendor applies appropriate security controls — controls the organisation cannot directly observe or enforce. This loss of visibility is the core third-party risk. Encryption and MFA are available in virtually all enterprise cloud services; nation-state threat levels are not automatically increased.

Domain: 3.0 Security Architecture
Sub-objective: 3.1 — Compare and contrast security implications of different architecture models
Question: Software-defined networking (SDN) separates which two planes to enable centralised management?
A) Data plane and management plane
B) Control plane and data plane
C) Application plane and forwarding plane
D) Physical plane and virtual plane
Correct: B
Explanation: SDN separates the control plane (decision-making about how traffic is routed) from the data plane (actual packet forwarding). This separation allows centralised, programmable network management. The security implication is that the SDN controller becomes a high-value target — its compromise could affect the entire network.

Domain: 3.0 Security Architecture
Sub-objective: 3.1 — Compare and contrast security implications of different architecture models
Question: An organisation runs critical applications on-premises but uses public cloud for burst capacity during peak loads. This is BEST described as:
A) Community cloud
B) Multi-cloud
C) Hybrid cloud
D) Private cloud extension
Correct: C
Explanation: Hybrid cloud combines on-premises (private) infrastructure with public cloud resources, often connected by a secure network link. Multi-cloud refers to using multiple public cloud providers. The key indicator here is the combination of on-premises and public cloud.

Domain: 3.0 Security Architecture
Sub-objective: 3.1 — Compare and contrast security implications of different architecture models
Question: Which of the following BEST characterises a security risk specific to IoT devices?
A) They always require high-bandwidth network connections
B) They commonly lack update mechanisms and ship with hardcoded credentials
C) They are typically managed through centralised identity providers
D) They use proprietary encryption that is difficult for attackers to break
Correct: B
Explanation: IoT devices frequently ship with default or hardcoded credentials and lack robust firmware update mechanisms, making long-term patching infeasible. This combination leaves devices persistently vulnerable. They do not typically integrate with enterprise identity providers and do not use particularly strong proprietary encryption.

Domain: 3.0 Security Architecture
Sub-objective: 3.1 — Compare and contrast security implications of different architecture models
Question: SCADA systems used in industrial environments present unique security challenges because they:
A) Were originally designed with security as the primary requirement
B) Often run legacy protocols that were not designed for IP networks or authenticated communications
C) Cannot be connected to any IP network by regulatory requirement
D) Use real-time operating systems that automatically patch vulnerabilities
Correct: B
Explanation: SCADA and ICS systems were originally designed for isolated, air-gapped environments with proprietary protocols (e.g., Modbus, DNP3) that lack authentication and encryption. As these systems have been connected to IP networks for remote management, their original design assumptions have become critical vulnerabilities.

Domain: 3.0 Security Architecture
Sub-objective: 3.1 — Compare and contrast security implications of different architecture models
Question: A Real-Time Operating System (RTOS) used in an embedded medical device differs from a general-purpose OS in that it prioritises:
A) Feature richness and user interface complexity
B) Deterministic timing and response guarantees over security features
C) Frequent security patches and updates
D) Integration with cloud identity management services
Correct: B
Explanation: RTOS systems are designed to guarantee predictable, deterministic timing — critical for safety in medical, aviation, or industrial contexts. Security features like memory isolation, ASLR, or dynamic patching are often sacrificed to meet real-time constraints. This makes RTOS devices difficult to secure using traditional methods.

Domain: 3.0 Security Architecture
Sub-objective: 3.1 — Compare and contrast security implications of different architecture models
Question: Which architectural approach BEST reduces the attack surface exposed when an application is compromised?
A) Centralised monolithic architecture
B) Decentralised microservices with least-privilege inter-service communication
C) Shared-nothing architecture with a single administrative domain
D) Serverless architecture without network access controls
Correct: B
Explanation: Decentralised microservices with least-privilege communication limits what an attacker can reach after compromising one service. A monolithic architecture means a single compromise can expose the entire application. Serverless without network controls can increase exposure. The key principle is blast radius reduction through segmentation and least privilege.

Domain: 3.0 Security Architecture
Sub-objective: 3.1 — Compare and contrast security implications of different architecture models
Question: A PaaS environment allows developers to deploy applications without managing servers. The PRIMARY residual security responsibility for the developer is:
A) Hypervisor configuration
B) Physical data centre access controls
C) Application code security and data protection
D) Network routing table management
Correct: C
Explanation: In PaaS, the provider manages infrastructure through the runtime. The developer is responsible for secure application code (e.g., avoiding injection vulnerabilities) and protecting the data the application processes. Hypervisor, physical controls, and network routing are all provider-managed in PaaS.

Domain: 3.0 Security Architecture
Sub-objective: 3.1 — Compare and contrast security implications of different architecture models
Question: A security architect is evaluating risks of using a public cloud provider. Which of the following represents a UNIQUE risk not present with on-premises infrastructure?
A) Physical hardware failures
B) Network latency between services
C) Multi-tenant data isolation failures
D) Software vulnerabilities in the OS
Correct: C
Explanation: Multi-tenancy — multiple customers sharing the same physical infrastructure — creates a risk unique to public cloud: a vulnerability in the hypervisor or container runtime could allow one tenant to access another's data. Physical hardware failures, network latency, and OS vulnerabilities all exist in on-premises environments too.

Domain: 3.0 Security Architecture
Sub-objective: 3.1 — Compare and contrast security implications of different architecture models
Question: Which of the following is an advantage of a decentralised architecture from a resilience and security perspective?
A) Simpler access control administration
B) No single point of failure or compromise
C) Reduced need for encryption between components
D) Easier log aggregation and monitoring
Correct: B
Explanation: Decentralised architectures distribute authority and resources so that no single node or component represents a single point of failure or a single point of compromise. The tradeoffs are complexity in administration and monitoring — making A and D incorrect. Encryption needs increase, not decrease, in decentralised environments.

Domain: 3.0 Security Architecture
Sub-objective: 3.1 — Compare and contrast security implications of different architecture models
Question: A company's security team discovers that a container image in use contains a critical OS-level vulnerability. Which mitigation is MOST appropriate for containerised environments?
A) Patch the running container in place
B) Rebuild the container image with an updated base image and redeploy
C) Migrate the workload to a virtual machine
D) Apply a host-based firewall rule to the container
Correct: B
Explanation: Containers are treated as immutable — rather than patching a running container, the correct approach is to rebuild the image from a patched base image and redeploy. This is a core principle of container security and IaC: replace rather than repair. Host-based firewalls address network exposure, not OS vulnerabilities.

Domain: 3.0 Security Architecture
Sub-objective: 3.1 — Compare and contrast security implications of different architecture models
Question: An organisation wants to ensure that workloads running in a public cloud meet the same security standards as their on-premises environment. Which approach BEST achieves this?
A) Rely on the cloud provider's default security settings
B) Implement policy-as-code enforced through automated compliance checks in the CI/CD pipeline
C) Perform annual manual audits of cloud configuration
D) Restrict all cloud workloads to a single region
Correct: B
Explanation: Policy-as-code, enforced through automated checks in the CI/CD pipeline, ensures that every deployment is validated against security standards before reaching production. Annual manual audits are too infrequent for dynamic cloud environments. Default settings rarely meet enterprise security requirements. Single-region restriction addresses sovereignty, not security standards.

Domain: 3.0 Security Architecture
Sub-objective: 3.1 — Compare and contrast security implications of different architecture models
Question: A scenario: A financial services firm deploys trading algorithms on embedded systems with RTOS. A security audit finds that the RTOS cannot support standard endpoint protection agents. What is the MOST appropriate compensating control?
A) Replace all RTOS devices with general-purpose Linux servers
B) Implement network-based monitoring and strict network segmentation around the RTOS environment
C) Disable all network interfaces on the RTOS devices
D) Install a lightweight antivirus agent on each RTOS device
Correct: B
Explanation: When endpoint agents cannot be installed (a common RTOS constraint), compensating controls shift to the network layer: monitoring traffic to and from RTOS devices and isolating them in tightly controlled network segments. Disabling network interfaces may not be operationally feasible for trading systems. Replacing hardware is expensive and may not meet real-time requirements.

Domain: 3.0 Security Architecture
Sub-objective: 3.2 — Apply security principles to secure enterprise infrastructure
Question: Which protocol provides secure remote management of network devices by encrypting the entire session, including authentication?
A) Telnet
B) SNMPv1
C) SSH
D) TFTP
Correct: C
Explanation: SSH (Secure Shell) encrypts the entire session including credentials and commands. Telnet transmits everything in plaintext. SNMPv1 lacks authentication and encryption. TFTP (Trivial FTP) provides no authentication or encryption and is unsuitable for remote management.

Domain: 3.0 Security Architecture
Sub-objective: 3.2 — Apply security principles to secure enterprise infrastructure
Question: A company needs to protect DNS queries and responses from tampering and spoofing. Which protocol should be implemented?
A) DNSSEC
B) LDAPS
C) FTPS
D) SNMPv3
Correct: A
Explanation: DNSSEC (DNS Security Extensions) adds cryptographic signatures to DNS records, allowing resolvers to verify that responses have not been tampered with. It protects against DNS spoofing and cache poisoning. LDAPS secures LDAP directory queries; FTPS secures file transfers; SNMPv3 secures network device management.

Domain: 3.0 Security Architecture
Sub-objective: 3.2 — Apply security principles to secure enterprise infrastructure
Question: A network administrator wants to replace SNMPv1 for device monitoring to gain authentication and encryption. Which protocol is the appropriate replacement?
A) SNMP with community strings
B) SNMPv2c
C) SNMPv3
D) ICMP
Correct: C
Explanation: SNMPv3 adds authentication (using HMAC-MD5 or HMAC-SHA) and optional encryption (DES or AES) to SNMP. SNMPv1 and SNMPv2c rely on plaintext community strings for authentication, which are trivially intercepted. ICMP is a diagnostic protocol, not a management protocol.

Domain: 3.0 Security Architecture
Sub-objective: 3.2 — Apply security principles to secure enterprise infrastructure
Question: Which of the following BEST describes the purpose of a jump server (bastion host)?
A) To load balance traffic across multiple web servers
B) To provide a single, hardened access point into a secure network segment
C) To filter malicious traffic using signature-based detection
D) To terminate VPN connections from remote users
Correct: B
Explanation: A jump server is a hardened, monitored system that serves as the sole access point for administrators entering a secure or sensitive network zone. All administrative traffic routes through it, enabling centralised logging and access control. It is not a load balancer, IDS, or VPN concentrator.

Domain: 3.0 Security Architecture
Sub-objective: 3.2 — Apply security principles to secure enterprise infrastructure
Question: A network-based intrusion detection system (NIDS) is placed at which location to provide MAXIMUM visibility into traffic?
A) On each endpoint's loopback interface
B) Behind the firewall, monitoring only internal traffic
C) At a network tap or span port that mirrors all traffic crossing a key segment
D) Inside the application server's operating system
Correct: C
Explanation: A NIDS requires visibility into network traffic, achieved by mirroring traffic to it via a network tap or switch SPAN port. Placement at a key boundary (e.g., between internet and internal network, or at a DMZ boundary) provides maximum visibility. Loopback and OS-level placement are host-based approaches.

Domain: 3.0 Security Architecture
Sub-objective: 3.2 — Apply security principles to secure enterprise infrastructure
Question: A stateful firewall differs from a packet filter firewall in that it:
A) Operates at Layer 7 and inspects application payloads
B) Tracks the state of network connections and allows return traffic automatically
C) Requires a proxy to intercept all connections
D) Can only filter traffic based on IP address ranges
Correct: B
Explanation: A stateful firewall maintains a connection state table, tracking TCP/UDP sessions. Return traffic for established connections is automatically permitted without needing explicit rules. A packet filter evaluates each packet independently. Layer 7 inspection is characteristic of next-generation or application firewalls, not stateful firewalls.

Domain: 3.0 Security Architecture
Sub-objective: 3.2 — Apply security principles to secure enterprise infrastructure
Question: Which type of firewall provides the DEEPEST inspection of application traffic, including the ability to identify and block specific application content?
A) Packet filter
B) Stateful inspection firewall
C) Next-generation firewall (NGFW)
D) Circuit-level gateway
Correct: C
Explanation: A next-generation firewall (NGFW) combines stateful inspection with deep packet inspection (DPI), application awareness, user identity tracking, and integrated IPS. Circuit-level gateways verify TCP handshakes without inspecting content. Packet filters and stateful firewalls do not inspect application-layer content.

Domain: 3.0 Security Architecture
Sub-objective: 3.2 — Apply security principles to secure enterprise infrastructure
Question: SRTP is used to provide confidentiality and authentication for which type of traffic?
A) Web application sessions
B) Real-time voice and video communications
C) Encrypted file transfers
D) LDAP directory queries
Correct: B
Explanation: SRTP (Secure Real-time Transport Protocol) provides encryption, message authentication, and replay protection for RTP streams — the protocol used to carry voice and video in VoIP and video conferencing. It is not used for web sessions (TLS handles those), file transfers, or directory queries.

Domain: 3.0 Security Architecture
Sub-objective: 3.2 — Apply security principles to secure enterprise infrastructure
Question: A security engineer needs to ensure that email messages are encrypted and digitally signed end-to-end between senders and recipients. Which protocol achieves this?
A) TLS
B) DKIM
C) S/MIME
D) STARTTLS
Correct: C
Explanation: S/MIME (Secure/Multipurpose Internet Mail Extensions) provides end-to-end encryption and digital signing of email content using public key certificates. TLS and STARTTLS protect the SMTP transport session between mail servers but not the message content itself. DKIM signs email headers to verify the sending domain, not message content.

Domain: 3.0 Security Architecture
Sub-objective: 3.2 — Apply security principles to secure enterprise infrastructure
Question: An organisation wants to secure LDAP communications between its application servers and Active Directory. Which configuration achieves this?
A) Enable SNMPv3 on the domain controllers
B) Configure LDAPS (LDAP over SSL/TLS) on port 636
C) Enable Kerberos authentication on port 389
D) Use SSH tunnelling for all LDAP queries
Correct: B
